import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a transposed 1D convolution operation with asymmetric input and square kernel.
    Supports padding, striding, and dilation.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the square convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        dilation (int, optional): Spacing between kernel elements. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, dilation: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv1d_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the transposed 1D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, length).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, length_out).
        """
        return self.conv1d_transpose(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 16
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 64
kernel_size = 3
# long sequence
length = 131072
stride = 2
padding = 1
dilation = 2

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, length)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation]

This is equivalent to upsampling by stride ( inserting zeros between input samples ) and then doing a standard convolution with stride 1. Specifically, for transposed conv with stride s, dilation d, kernel size k, padding p: We can think of it as: create an upsampled input of length L_in * stride (but with zeros inserted), then convolve with kernel of size (kernel_size-1)*dilation + 1, stride 1, and then crop. But the actual PyTorch implementation uses the gradient of conv as transposed conv. But manually, we can implement it as: for each output position, sum over kernel positions where the input contributes. Another way: for each input element x[b, c_in, i], it contributes to output channels c_out via weight[c_out, c_in, k]. This is the same as the forward of conv_transpose. So we can write a kernel that loops over output positions and sums contributions. But L_out = 262145, batch=16, out_c=64, in_c=32, kernel=3. Actually, for fp32, each address is 4 bytes. A cache line is 128 bytes. Stride 2 means threads write 8 bytes apart, so 16 threads cover 128 bytes, which is exactly one cache line? Actually, yes: within a warp of 32 threads, they cover 256 bytes (two cache lines). The hardware can coalesce these into two transactions. So strided writes with step 2 are coalesced as long as the stride is a power of 2 and the warp covers a contiguous block of memory. We need floor. # We can compute conservatively: i_min_needed = max(0, (j_start + padding - (K-1)*dilation) / stride - 1) to be safe? Or we can compute exactly. # Simpler: just use a slightly wider range. i_min_needed = max(0, (j_start + padding - (K-1)*dilation) / stride - 1); i_max_needed = min(L_in - 1, (j_end + padding) / stride + 1); # Load x tile into shared memory: # for tid in range(0, x_tile_size, block_size): x_shared[tid] = x[b, c_in, i_min_needed + tid] # Wait, x_shared needs to hold [in_c][i_count]. So size = in_c * i_count. # After loading x_shared, each thread loops over c_in and k: # j = j_start + threadIdx.x # sum = 0 # for c_in in 0..in_c-1: # for k in 0..K-1: # if ((j + padding - k*d) % stride == 0): # i = (j + padding - k*d) / stride # if (0 <= i < L_in): # x_val = x_shared[c_in, i - i_min_needed] # w_val = weight[c_out, c_in, k] # sum += x_val * w_val # out[b, c_out, j] = sum + bias This requires computing i_min_needed and i_max_needed. The tile_j can be chosen such that i_count is manageable. For our parameters: j_start = blockIdx.x * tile_j. j_end = j_start + tile_j - 1. i_min_needed = max(0, (j_start + 1 - 4) / 2 - 1) = max(0, (j_start - 3)/2 - 1). i_max_needed = min(L_in-1, (j_end + 1)/2 + 1) ≈ j_end/2 + 1. So i_count ≈ (j_end - j_start)/2 + few = tile_j/2 + few. If tile_j = 128, i_count ≈ 64 + 4 = 68. x_shared size = 32 * 68 = 2176 floats = 8.7KB. Good. w_shared: we can also load weight for the block's c_out tile. tile_c_out = 8 => 8*32*3 = 768 floats = 3KB. Total = ~12KB. Block size = tile_j * tile_c_out = 128 * 8 = 1024 threads. But we need to be careful about the modulo and division in the kernel. Since K is small, the branch is okay. However, for our case, we know that for j odd, all k are valid (modulo always 0). For j even, no k is valid. So the inner loop would be skipped for even j. Since half the j in the tile are even, half the threads in a warp do nothing. That's 50% waste. We can fix this by launching only over odd j. Since odd j are j = 2*t+1. So we can define t_start = blockIdx.x * tile_t, and j = 2*(t_start + threadIdx.x) + 1. Then threadIdx.x maps to t. tile_t = tile_j/2 if we want same number of threads per block. Then tile_j effective = 2*tile_t. The i range needed for t in [t_start, t_start+tile_t-1] is i ≈ t_start+1 - (K-1) to t_start+tile_t. So contiguous. But for generic parameters, we don't know the parity. However, we can compute the set of valid j's more generally. Actually, we can compute a stride step for valid j's. Since the condition for any k to be valid is that there exists k such that (j + p - k*d) % s == 0 and i in range. The set of j modulo s that are valid is the set of residues r_k = (k*d - p) mod s for which there exists some i in range. If s is small (e.g., <= kernel_size), there are at most K valid residues. For large s, there might be many, but K is small. In the worst case, each k gives a distinct residue class. Then there are K residue classes. We can process each residue class separately. But that's a lot of code. Given the time, I think the best approach is: 1. Write a generic gather kernel that processes all j, but for the specific test case (stride>1) we can include an optimization: if stride > 1 and all k share the same residue class (i.e., (k*dilation) % stride is constant for all k), then we can step by stride. This is true when dilation % stride == 0 (as in our case, 2%2=0) or when K is small and the values coincide by chance. But it's not necessary for correctness. 2. Alternatively, write a generic scatter kernel (over i) with atomicAdd. This is very general and always has 100% thread utilization (except boundaries). The only downside is atomicAdd. But as argued, contention is low. Which is easier to implement correctly and likely to be fast? The scatter kernel. Let's design the scatter kernel carefully. Kernel signature: __global__ void conv_transpose1d( const float* x, const float* w, float* out, int batch, int in_c, int L_in, int out_c, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation) Grid: dim3(ceil_div(L_in, tile_i), ceil_div(out_c, tile_c_out), batch) Block: dim3(tile_i, tile_c_out, 1) Each thread: int i = blockIdx.x * tile_i + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * tile_c_out + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (i >= L_in || c_out >= out_c) return; float accum[KERNEL_MAX]; // K=3, we can use a fixed max like 8 or dynamic. For k=0..K-1: accum[k] = 0.0f; // Loop over c_in for (int c_in = 0; c_in < in_c; ++c_in) { float x_val = x[((b * in_c + c_in) * L_in) + i]; // Unroll K? K is small. for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { float w_val = w[((c_out * in_c + c_in) * K) + k]; accum[k] += x_val * w_val; } } // Write to output for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int j = i * stride + k * dilation - padding; if (j >= 0 && j < L_out) { atomicAdd(&out[((b * out_c + c_out) * L_out) + j], accum[k]); } } This kernel is simple. But it does atomicAdd for each k. Is there a way to reduce the number of atomicAdds? We could accumulate across c_in into a local array, then write. That's what we do. But note: each thread writes to K output positions. For K=3, it's 3 atomics per thread. For all threads, that's 3 * batch * out_c * L_in atomics ≈ 402M atomics. On A100, atomicAdd throughput for fp32 is roughly 100-200 Gops/s? Actually, global atomic throughput is lower than memory bandwidth, maybe ~50-100B updates/s? Let's approximate. 402M atomics is not huge. At 50B/s, it would take ~8ms. The total compute is ~12.9B FMAs. At 10 TFLOPS, that's ~1.3ms. So atomicAdd could dominate. To improve, we can note that for interior i, the j values are always valid. Only near boundaries do we need bounds checks. For our case, L_in = 131072, K=3, stride=2, dilation=2, padding=1. j = 2i + 2k - 1. For i=0: j = -1, 1, 3. So k=0 invalid. For i=1: j = 1, 3, 5. All valid if L_out >=6. L_out=262145, so yes. For i=L_in-1 = 131071: j = 262141, 262143, 262145. k=2 invalid since j>=L_out. For i=L_in-2: j = 262139, 262141, 262143. All valid. So only the first i (i=0) has one invalid k, and the last i (i=131071) has one invalid k. All other i have all 3 valid. So we can handle boundaries specially. We can split the kernel into two parts: interior and boundary. Or just handle the check; the warp divergence is minimal (only warps at the block boundaries might have some divergence). But the atomicAdd overhead remains. What if we process tile_i such that we accumulate over c_in and then use block-level reduction or shared memory to combine writes? Not helpful. What if we do not use atomicAdd, but instead have each block compute a partial output tile and then write it to global memory? We can have each block write to its own output buffer, and then sum them with a reduction kernel. That would be two passes. Not ideal. Alternatively, we can interleave the reduction by assigning threads to (b, c_out, i_mod, c_in)? No. Wait, what if we transpose the loops: process (b, c_out, j) by assigning each thread to one j, but we process i in tiles and keep a partial sum in registers, reading x and w from shared memory. That is the gather with tiling approach. Let's revisit that. Gather kernel with tiling: Grid: dim3(ceil_div(L_out, tile_j), ceil_div(out_c, tile_c_out), batch) Block: dim3(tile_j, tile_c_out, 1) Each thread handles one (b, c_out, j). j = blockIdx.x * tile_j + threadIdx.x; c_out = ... b = ... if (j >= L_out) return; // Determine i range needed for this block's j tile int j_start = blockIdx.x * tile_j; int j_end = min(j_start + tile_j, L_out) - 1; int i_min = max(0, (j_start + padding - (K-1)*dilation) / stride - 1); int i_max = min(L_in - 1, (j_end + padding) / stride + 1); int i_count = i_max - i_min + 1; // Load x for this b and i_min..i_max into shared memory // x_shared shape: [in_c][i_count] (or [i_count][in_c]) // We need to load in_c * i_count floats. // Each thread loads a portion. for (int idx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * tile_j; idx < in_c * i_count; idx += tile_j * tile_c_out) { int c = idx / i_count; int i_local = idx % i_count; int i_global = i_min + i_local; x_shared[c * i_count + i_local] = x[((b * in_c + c) * L_in) + i_global]; } __syncthreads(); // Load weight for this c_out into registers or shared memory // w_shared shape: [tile_c_out][in_c][K] for (int idx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * tile_j; idx < tile_c_out * in_c * K; idx += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { // compute c_out_local, c_in, k int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % in_c; tmp /= in_c; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = blockIdx.y * tile_c_out + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < out_c) { w_shared[idx] = w[((c_out_global * in_c + c_in) * K) + k]; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute float sum = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < in_c; ++c_in) { for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int numerator = j + padding - k * dilation; if (numerator % stride == 0) { int i = numerator / stride; if (i >= i_min && i <= i_max) { float x_val = x_shared[c_in * i_count + (i - i_min)]; float w_val = w_shared[(threadIdx.y * in_c + c_in) * K + k]; sum += x_val * w_val; } } } } // bias out[((b * out_c + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum; This kernel writes to output once per thread, no atomics. But the modulo and branch might cause divergence and overhead. For our case, j + 1 - 2k is always even for odd j, odd for even j. So the branch is perfectly divergent within a warp if tile_j contains both even and odd j. We can fix this by choosing tile_j to be all even or all odd? Not contiguous. Alternatively, we can precompute on CPU a list of valid (j, k, i) triples or just skip the modulo inside the kernel by processing j in steps of stride. For the test case, we can step j by 2. Generalization: For a given block, the set of j in [j_start, j_end] can be partitioned into residue classes modulo stride. But within a block, threads have contiguous threadIdx.x. We can have threadIdx.x not correspond to consecutive j, but to j = j_start + threadIdx.x * stride + r, where r is a chosen residue. If we choose r to be the residue class that is most common (or the only one with valid k), then all threads in the block process valid j, and there is no modulo branch! This is powerful. Let's formalize: On the CPU side (in Python), before launching the kernel, we can compute the set of residues modulo stride that are valid. For each valid residue r, we launch a separate kernel (or use a grid stride) to process j = r + t*stride. Since the output positions for a given residue are independent and uniformly spaced by stride. Then inside the kernel, for a given j, we can compute i for each k without modulo check: i = (j + padding - k*dilation) / stride. But we still need to check if i is in [0, L_in-1] (boundary check), and also we need to ensure that j is indeed valid for this k. Wait, if we group by residue, we need to group by the residue of j for a particular k? Actually, for a fixed j, multiple k might be valid only if they share the same residue. If they have different residues, then a given j cannot be valid for multiple k. But in our case, they share the same residue. So if we group j by residue class, we can process all j in that class. For each such j, we need to loop over k that have that residue. For our case, all 3 k have the same residue, so all j in that class use all 3 k. In general, for each residue class r, let K_r be the list of k such that (k*dilation - padding) % stride == r. Then for j with j % stride == r, the valid k are exactly K_r. So we can process each residue class with a separate kernel launch, using only the k in K_r. How to do this in a single kernel launch? We can have the CPU compute the number of valid residue classes C (1 <= C <= stride). Then launch C kernels, or launch one kernel with an extra loop over classes? But that increases kernel launch overhead. Since stride is usually small (like 1,2,3,4), overhead is small. However, the assignment wants a single kernel, but we can call it C times from Python. Or we can write a kernel that takes an array of residues and processes all of them in a single launch by mapping blockIdx.x to the global index across all classes. For example, total_work = sum_r ceil(num_j_r / tile_j). Then in the kernel, compute which class and which tile. But we can also just use a generic gather kernel with all j, and rely on cuDNN-like speed. Given the complexity, maybe the atomicAdd scatter kernel is good enough. Let's test both if possible. Since we have limited tool calls (50) and turns (15), we need to be efficient. I propose: 1. Write a custom scatter kernel with atomicAdds (simple, generic). 2. Compile and correctness check. 3. Profile it. 4. If not fast enough, try the gather kernel with shared memory for x and w, and no atomics. 5. Try to optimize for the specific case (skip even j). Let's start with the simple scatter kernel and see how it performs. But wait, we need to define `ModelNew` in Python that loads the CUDA code via `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. The kernel needs to be a C++ function callable from Python. The C++ function can launch the CUDA kernel. Let's draft the initial kernel. Note: We need to handle fp32 precision. The weight and bias are parameters of the model. We need to store them in `ModelNew`. The reference model includes `self.conv1d_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose1d(...)`. In `ModelNew.__init__`, we will accept the same arguments and create the weight and bias tensors (or not create bias if False). Then in forward, we call our custom kernel on the input and weight. Wait, we can actually reuse the weight initialization of the reference? The evaluator expects `ModelNew` to accept same constructor arguments. It will instantiate `ModelNew(*get_init_inputs())`. The forward will be called with `get_inputs()`. The reference model is also instantiated. The evaluator will compare outputs and measure time. So we need to initialize the weight and bias in ModelNew to match the reference. We can initialize using `nn.ConvTranspose1d` and copy its weight and bias? Or we can initialize manually using `torch.nn.init.kaiming_uniform_` or whatever PyTorch uses. But the evaluator might not check initialization; it only checks forward output for given inputs. Wait, the test code uses `torch.rand` for input, but the model parameters are initialized randomly. The evaluator will compare outputs of Model and ModelNew. So ModelNew must have the same parameters as Model. How to ensure that? We can initialize `self.conv1d_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose1d(...)` in ModelNew's __init__, and then in forward call our custom kernel using `self.conv1d_transpose.weight` and `self.conv1d_transpose.bias`. That way the weights and biases are exactly the same (initialized by the same nn.Module). Yes! That's the easiest. So ModelNew can contain the reference module just to hold parameters, but not use it in forward. That's allowed. So: class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, bias=False): super().__init__() self.conv1d_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias) # compile custom kernel self.custom_op = load_inline(...) def forward(self, x): return self.custom_op(x, self.conv1d_transpose.weight, self.conv1d_transpose.bias, stride, padding, dilation) The custom op can be a C++ function that takes tensors and parameters, launches the CUDA kernel, and returns the output. We need to compute L_out. We can compute it in Python or C++. L_out = (x.size(2) - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + 1 But if there's output_padding (default 0). The reference uses output_padding=0. So we can compute L_out in Python and pass it to the kernel as an argument. Now, writing the C++ CUDA extension. We need to be careful about torch tensor data pointers and sizes. C++ function signature: torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor weight, torch::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride, int padding, int dilation) Constraints: x is [N, C_in, L_in], weight is [C_out, C_in, K], bias is [C_out] or None. Compute L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (K - 1) + 1. Output: [N, C_out, L_out]. Initialize output to zeros. Then launch kernel. Let's write the scatter kernel first. CUDA kernel: template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_scatter_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { int i = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (i >= L_in || c_out >= C_out) return; // Optionally preload weight for this c_out into registers? Weight is small but not tiny. // For each k and c_in, we need w[c_out, c_in, k]. // We can load w into a local array: float w_local[C_in * K]; // 32*3=96 floats = 384 bytes per thread. That's a lot of registers. // Better to read from global memory each time (cached in L1). // Or we can use shared memory for weight tile. // Loop over c_in for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { float x_val = x[(b * C_in + c_in) * L_in + i]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { float w_val = w[(c_out * C_in + c_in) * K + k]; int j = i * stride + k * dilation - padding; if (j >= 0 && j < L_out) { atomicAdd(&out[(b * C_out + c_out) * L_out + j], x_val * w_val); } } } } Wait, this does atomicAdd inside the c_in loop. That means for each c_in (32) and each k (3), we do an atomicAdd. Total atomics per thread = 96. That's a lot. The accum should be per k across c_in to reduce atomics. Corrected: float accum[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) accum[k] = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { float x_val = x[(b * C_in + c_in) * L_in + i]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { float w_val = w[(c_out * C_in + c_in) * K + k]; accum[k] += x_val * w_val; } } #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int j = i * stride + k * dilation - padding; if (j >= 0 && j < L_out) { atomicAdd(&out[(b * C_out + c_out) * L_out + j], accum[k]); } } Now atomics per thread = K = 3. Much better. But we can reduce it further by handling boundaries only for the first and last i in the entire tensor, not per thread. However, each thread handles a specific i, so it must check j bounds unless we know it's interior. Since L_in is large (131072), only i=0 has one invalid j (k=0, j=-1). i=1..L_in-2 have all valid. i=L_in-1 has one invalid j (k=2, j=262145). So the branch is mostly uniform within a warp except at the edges. Can we split the kernel into three regions: i=0, i=1..L_in-2, i=L_in-1? We could launch separate kernels or have the host handle the boundary elements. But that's micro-optimization. Another optimization: we can avoid atomicAdd entirely by noting that for this specific case, j for different i never overlap if we process only one k per block? Actually, j overlap arises from different i mapping to same j for different k. But a single (i,k) pair maps to a unique j. So if we assign each thread to a unique (i,k) pair, then writes are to unique j (no conflicts). Then we need to sum over c_in. But we also need to sum over different (i,k) pairs that map to the same j. So we still need to combine contributions from different threads. But what if we process the reduction over c_in using a block-level reduction, and then each block writes to a unique set of j's? This is exactly the gather approach. Given the potential slowness of atomicAdd, let's try the gather approach with shared memory. Gather kernel with shared memory: template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_gather_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { int j = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (j >= L_out || c_out >= C_out) return; // Shared memory for x tile and weight tile extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_smem = smem; // [C_in][i_count] // w_smem: after x_smem // We'll dynamically size. int j_start = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x; int j_end = min(j_start + blockDim.x, L_out) - 1; // Compute i range // Conservative range: int i_min = max(0, (j_start + padding - (K - 1) * dilation) / stride - 1); int i_max = min(L_in - 1, (j_end + padding) / stride + 1); // Actually, we need floor division for potentially negative numbers. // In C++ integer division truncates toward zero. For negative, we need to be careful. // Since (j_start + padding - (K-1)*dilation) could be negative (if j_start=0, padding=1, K=3, d=2 => 1 - 4 = -3). // Using / with negative will give 0 for -3/2 = -1 (trunc toward zero). But floor(-1.5) = -2. So -3/2 = -1, which is greater than floor. So i_min would be -1, but we max with 0, so it's fine. // However, for safety, we can subtract an extra 1. // ... int i_count = i_max - i_min + 1; if (i_count < 0) i_count = 0; // Load x into shared memory // x_smem layout: [C_in][i_count] or [i_count][C_in]? For coalesced load, we want adjacent threads to access adjacent memory. // We have tile_j threads along x. Let's have thread idx t = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x. // To load x, we can loop over c_in and i_local. // x global address: x[b, c_in, i_global] = x[((b*C_in + c_in)*L_in) + i_global] // For fixed c_in and varying i_global, addresses are contiguous. // So we can have each warp load a contiguous chunk of i for a given c_in. // We'll use a linearized loop. int block_size = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; int total_x = C_in * i_count; for (int idx = tidx; idx < total_x; idx += block_size) { int c_in = idx / i_count; int i_local = idx % i_count; int i_global = i_min + i_local; x_smem[c_in * i_count + i_local] = x[((b * C_in + c_in) * L_in) + i_global]; } // Load weight into shared memory int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y; float* w_smem = x_smem + total_x; int total_w = blockDim.y * C_in * K; // tile_c_out * C_in * K for (int idx = tidx; idx < total_w; idx += block_size) { int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < C_out) { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k]; } else { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int numerator = j + padding - k * dilation; if (numerator % stride == 0) { int i = numerator / stride; if (i >= i_min && i <= i_max) { float x_val = x_smem[c_in * i_count + (i - i_min)]; float w_val = w_smem[(threadIdx.y * C_in + c_in) * K + k]; sum += x_val * w_val; } } } } // Add bias if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[c_out]; } out[((b * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum; } The shared memory size is dynamic: (C_in * i_count + blockDim.y * C_in * K) * sizeof(float). For our parameters, tile_j = 64, tile_c_out = 8: i_count ≈ 64/2 + 2 = 34. total_x = 32*34 = 1088. total_w = 8*32*3 = 768. total = 1856 floats = 7.4KB. Block size = 512. 7.4KB shared mem. Occupancy should be high. But we have the modulo and branch. Can we unroll and specialize K? K is template parameter. For our case, stride=2, dilation=2, padding=1, K=3. The branch is: numerator = j + 1 - 2k. k=0: j+1. divisible by 2 if j odd. k=1: j-1. divisible by 2 if j odd. k=2: j-3. divisible by 2 if j odd. So for even j, all k are skipped. For odd j, all k are taken. To avoid the modulo, we could process only odd j. In the kernel, we can skip even j by having the host launch fewer blocks and map indices accordingly. Idea: host computes the set of valid output indices for the "most common" residue class. But to keep generic, maybe we can just accept the branch. The branch is uniform for odd j blocks and even j blocks. If we process contiguous j, a warp covers 32 j's (16 odd, 16 even). So the warp executes the k loop for half the threads. That's 50% utilization. What if we launch threads only for odd j? We can set: int j_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int j = 2 * j_idx + 1; // only odd j Then we only need L_out_odd = (L_out + 1) / 2 threads per c_out. j_start = 2*(blockIdx.x * blockDim.x) + 1. j_end = j_start + 2*(blockDim.x-1) + 1 = j_start + 2*blockDim.x - 1. i_min = max(0, (j_start + padding - (K-1)*dilation) / stride - 1) i_max = min(L_in - 1, (j_end + padding) / stride + 1) i_count ≈ blockDim.x + few. This eliminates the modulo completely. But it only works if all k share the same residue class. In general, there might be multiple residue classes. We can handle the most common case where they share one class. But we need a generic fallback. How to detect if all k share the same residue class? Compute r0 = (0*dilation - padding) % stride. Actually, the residue class for k is (k*dilation - padding) mod stride. We can compute r_k for each k. If all r_k are equal, then all valid j belong to one residue class. This happens when dilation % stride == 0, or when K is small and the residues coincide. The test case has dilation=2, stride=2, so true. If not all equal, we could launch multiple passes, one per distinct residue class. But for simplicity, the generic kernel can just process all j. Actually, we can write a generic kernel that handles all j but unrolls the loop over k and uses a conditional that is likely to be warp-uniform for many j. For random parameters, it might be okay. For the benchmark, the performance matters. Given the time, I think the best is to implement the gather kernel with shared memory and a template parameter for K. We can compile multiple instantiations for common K values (e.g., K=1,2,3,4,5,7) inside the C++ code. Or we can just use a dynamic loop. But writing the shared memory sizing and loading is a bit tricky but manageable. Alternatively, we can precompute a small lookup table on CPU: for each k, compute the first j where it's valid, and then j increments by stride. Then in the kernel, we process each k separately: for each k, we have j range [j_k_start, j_k_end] with step stride. Then we can assign threads to (c_out, j_k). For each k, we launch a separate kernel or handle it in the same kernel by mapping the global thread index to a specific k. This is also complex. Let's step back and think about the simplest kernel that might still be fast: the scatter kernel with atomicAdd but using vectorized loads and shared memory for weight. Scatter kernel with shared memory for weight: - We load the weight for the block's c_out tile into shared memory. - Each thread loops over c_in, reads x from global memory, reads w from shared memory, accumulates into registers. - Then each thread writes K values via atomicAdd to global output. - To reduce atomic contention, we can have each thread write sequentially. Given that the output is initially zero, and each output element receives contributions from at most K blocks, the number of atomics is K per output element. For K=3, atomic count = 3 * 134M = 402M. On A100, global atomicAdd throughput is ~100-200 Gops/s? Actually, fp32 atomic throughput on A100 is around 200 Gops/s? Let's check typical numbers. For A100, global memory atomics throughput is roughly equal to L2 bandwidth, maybe ~3-4 TB/s aggregated? Wait, atomic operations on global memory go to L2 and are serialized per address. The throughput is limited by L2 caches. For scattered atomics (different addresses), throughput can be high, maybe approaching memory bandwidth. 402M atomics * 4 bytes = 1.6GB. That's tiny. The latency is more of an issue. But with many warps, we can hide latency. However, if many threads atomicAdd to addresses that are close together (same cache line), they will be serialized. In our case, for a fixed b, c_out, and k, the j's accessed by contiguous i are strided by stride=2. So they are 8 bytes apart (for fp32). Two adjacent threads access addresses 8 bytes apart, which fall into different 32-byte segments but share a 128-byte cache line. Actually, 8 bytes apart means 16 addresses per 128-byte line. So 16 threads in a warp write to the same cache line. Those 16 atomics will be serialized in the L2 cache. That's not great. If the warp has 32 threads, they write to two cache lines, and within each cache line, 16 atomics are serialized. So the warp takes ~16 cycles per atomic? Actually, the L2 can handle some concurrent atomics to different addresses in the same line, but there is serialization. This could be a bottleneck. To reduce serialization, we can have threads in a warp write to addresses that are farther apart. For example, if we assign threadIdx.x to c_out and threadIdx.y to i, then for a fixed i and k, threads in a warp write to output[b, c_out, j] with varying c_out. These addresses are separated by L_out * 4 bytes ≈ 1MB. So they are in different cache lines. No serialization. But then the x read is uniform (good), weight read is contiguous (good), but writes are scattered across memory. However, since they are to different cache lines, they can be processed in parallel by different L2 banks. This might be faster. But the mapping threadIdx.x to c_out means blockDim.x = tile_c_out. If tile_c_out = 64, blockDim.x = 64. Then blockDim.y = tile_i. For tile_i = 8, block = 512 threads. In this case, a warp of 32 threads has fixed i and varying c_out (if tile_c_out >= 32). So all threads in warp compute for the same i but different c_out. They read the same x (broadcast). They read contiguous w (coalesced). They write to j = i*stride + k*d - p with varying c_out. The writes for adjacent c_out are separated by L_out floats (1M). So they are far apart. Since each address gets only one atomic per block (actually per thread, but each thread writes a unique (c_out, j) pair), there is no address conflict across threads in the same warp! Each address is written by exactly one thread (for a given i and k). Wait, no: for a given j and c_out, there are multiple i's that contribute. Different blocks (different i) will atomicAdd to the same (c_out, j). But within a single block, each (c_out, j) pair is written at most once (since each block handles a disjoint set of i). So within the same block, there is no conflict across threads. Conflicts only happen across blocks that map different i to the same j. But those blocks are different and execute at different times (maybe concurrently). Their writes to the same address will be serialized. However, since there are at most K blocks writing to the same address (as discussed), and K=3, the serialization is limited. Now, within a warp, since each thread writes to a different (c_out, j) address (for a fixed i and k), there is no intra-warp conflict. This is great! Because even though the addresses are far apart, the atomic units in the GPU can handle them in parallel if they map to different L2 partitions. Thus the scatter kernel with threadIdx.x = c_out, threadIdx.y = i may be better than threadIdx.x = i, threadIdx.y = c_out. Let's choose: blockDim.x = tile_c_out = 64 (all output channels). blockDim.y = tile_i = 8. block = 512 threads. gridDim.x = 1 (since 64 covers out_c=64). gridDim.y = ceil(L_in / 8) = 16384. gridDim.z = batch = 16. Total blocks = 1 * 16384 * 16 = 262144. Each block processes 8 input positions and 64 output channels. Weight shared memory: tile_c_out * C_in * K = 64*32*3 = 6144 floats = 24KB. This can be loaded once per block and reused for all i in the block. X shared memory: we could also load x for the tile of i into shared memory, but it's small: tile_i * C_in = 8*32 = 256 floats = 1KB. Let's load it too. So total shared memory = 25KB. Block 512 threads. Occupancy: 512 threads * 3 blocks = 1536 per SM (75%). Good. But wait, weight shared memory is 24KB, which is a bit large but okay. If we have 48KB per SM, we can fit 2 blocks (48KB), giving 1024 threads per SM (50% occupancy). If SM has 96KB (A100), we can fit 3 blocks (1536 threads, 75% occupancy). So it's okay. Alternatively, we can avoid loading weight into shared memory and just read it from global memory. Since each warp reads weight for 32 c_out, which is contiguous and cached, L1/L2 might do fine. But shared memory is faster and reduces L1 pressure. Given that weight is only 24KB and is reused across all i in the block (8 i's) and all threads (512), it's highly beneficial to load it into shared memory. So the scatter kernel with shared memory for weight and x is good. Let s = stride, d = dilation, p = padding. For a given k, valid j satisfy j ≡ (k*d - p) (mod s). As k ranges 0..K-1, the right-hand side takes values r_k = (k*d - p) mod s. Some r_k may be duplicates. The set of valid j is the union over distinct r of { j | j ≡ r (mod s) and 0 ≤ (j + p - k*d)/s < L_in for some k with r_k = r }. For each residue class r, the valid j form a contiguous interval (possibly empty) with step s. Thus we can process each residue class separately with a kernel that steps j by s. This means we can write a generic gather kernel that processes one residue class at a time, launched from the host in a loop over distinct residues. The host computes the residues, j ranges, and the list of k's per residue. Then for each residue, we launch a kernel where each thread handles one j in that class. Then the kernel becomes: For each thread (global_tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x): c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; b = blockIdx.z; if (global_tid >= num_j) return; j = j_start + global_tid * stride; // or j_step float sum = 0.0f; for (c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = base_i[k] + global_tid; if (i >= 0 && i < L_in) { sum += x[((b*C_in + c_in)*L_in) + i] * w[((c_out*C_in + c_in)*K) + k]; } } } out[((b*C_out + c_out)*L_out) + j] = sum + bias; The i check is still needed for boundaries (global_tid near 0 or num_j-1). Wait, for interior t, all k are valid. For t near boundaries, some k are invalid because i goes out of [0, L_in-1]. For our case, t=0 corresponds to j=1. i for k=2 is -1 (invalid). So we can either let the kernel handle it with a branch (rare) or we can have the host shrink the range and handle boundaries separately. The branch is rare enough that it's fine. But the memory access pattern: for a fixed c_in, and a warp of 32 threads with contiguous global_tid, the i's are contiguous! So x reads are coalesced. Weight reads are uniform across the warp (same c_out, c_in, k). Perfect! This is a very efficient gather kernel. And we can use shared memory to cache weight (for the tile of c_out) and maybe x tile. Here is the plan: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { int j = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (j >= L_out || c_out >= C_out) return; // tile_j = blockDim.x int j_start = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x; int j_end = min(j_start + blockDim.x, L_out) - 1; // Determine approximate i range int i_min = (j_start + padding - (K - 1) * dilation) / stride - 1; int i_max = (j_end + padding) / stride + 1; if (i_min < 0) i_min = 0; if (i_max >= L_in) i_max = L_in - 1; int i_count = i_max - i_min + 1; if (i_count < 0) i_count = 0; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_smem = smem; float* w_smem = smem + C_in * i_count; int block_size = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; // Load x for (int idx = tidx; idx < C_in * i_count; idx += block_size) { int c_in = idx / i_count; int i_local = idx % i_count; int i_global = i_min + i_local; x_smem[c_in * i_count + i_local] = x[((b * C_in + c_in) * L_in) + i_global]; } // Load w int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y; int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K; for (int idx = tidx; idx < w_size; idx += block_size) { int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < C_out) { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k]; } else { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int num = j + padding - k * dilation; if (num % stride == 0) { int i = num / stride; if (i >= i_min && i <= i_max) { float x_val = x_smem[c_in * i_count + (i - i_min)]; float w_val = w_smem[(threadIdx.y * C_in + c_in) * K + k]; sum += x_val * w_val; } } } } if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[c_out]; } out[((b * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum; } ``` Wait, the modulo and branch inside the inner loop is expensive. For each j and k, we do `if ((j + p - k*d) % s == 0)`. With s=2, we can replace `% stride == 0` with `(j + p - k*d) & 1 == 0`. But the compiler might not do that automatically if stride is variable. However, the real issue is that half the warps' threads are active and half inactive in the same warp. For j even and j odd interleaved, a warp of 32 has 16 active threads. This means the kernel runs the compute path for 16 threads and the skip path for 16. The skip path is just a few branches; the compute path is 96 FMAs and shared memory reads. The warp executes the compute path (16 threads active). So the effective throughput is halved. That means we need twice as many warps to achieve the same FLOPS. Since we have many blocks, maybe it's okay. But the GPU might be able to issue instructions for the active threads every cycle, and the inactive threads don't consume execution resources? Actually, in SIMT, all threads in a warp execute the same instruction. If only 16 are active, the execution units are half-utilized for that instruction. So throughput is halved. This is significant. Is there a way to have a warp process only odd j? Yes, if we launch the grid such that threadIdx.x corresponds to j' = j/2 (or (j-1)/2). Then all threads in a warp are active. The j values are j = j_start + threadIdx.x * 2 + 1. This requires the host to know that the valid j's are odd. But the host can compute this! As discussed, the host can compute the progression step (`stride`) and offset (`first_valid_j`) if there is a single residue class. If there are multiple, we can launch multiple kernels. Given that the performance hit of 50% utilization is huge, we should implement the progression packing on the host. Okay, let's commit to the progression-based gather kernel. Implementation details in C++: We will write a templated kernel `conv_transpose1d_fast` that assumes all j in the progression are valid for all ks (interior). It also handles i bounds checks for safety (a cheap branch). Host side (C++ wrapper): - Compute L_out. - Initialize output to zeros (with bias handled separately if needed). - Determine the distinct residues and active ks. - For simplicity, if there is exactly 1 residue class and all ks are active in that class (which is the common case and definitely our test case), we launch a single kernel with j_step = stride, j_start computed as the first valid j in [0, L_out-1], and num_j computed as the count. - If there are multiple classes or partial ks, we fall back to a generic kernel that handles all j. Actually, to avoid writing the generic kernel and the progression detection in C++, we can do the progression detection in Python! Python can compute the valid j's and call the C++ kernel for the progression. But the C++ kernel is called via `load_inline`. The wrapper function is in C++ and takes tensors. We can have the C++ wrapper do all the logic. That's fine. But maybe it's simpler to just handle the generic case in C++ by always launching a generic gather kernel but with a different indexing to avoid the modulo. Wait, no. Alright, let's write two kernels in C++: 1. `conv_transpose1d_generic_kernel`: handles any j, loops over k, checks divisibility and bounds. 2. `conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel`: handles a single progression (step=stride), assumes all ks are valid, no modulo check. The wrapper function: - If `stride == 1`, call generic kernel (since all j are valid, no waste). - Else, compute if all ks share the same residue. If yes, compute j_start, num_j, and launch fast kernel for interior + generic kernel for boundaries? Or just fast kernel with bounds check. - If not, call generic kernel. For the test case, stride=2, all ks share residue. So fast kernel is used. Fast kernel design: ```cpp template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int j_base, int num_j) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; // index into progression int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (tid >= num_j || c_out >= C_out) return; int j = j_base + tid * stride; // Determine i0_k for each k // i_k = (j + padding - k*dilation) / stride // Since j = j_base + tid*stride, and (j_base + padding - k*dilation) is divisible by stride, // i_k = i_base[k] + tid. // We can compute i_base[k] once on host and pass it, or compute here. } ``` To avoid division in kernel, we can pass `int i_base[k]` as a constant array. But passing arrays to kernels is a bit verbose. Since K is small, we can pass `int i_base_0`, `int i_base_1`, etc. Or we can compute them on the fly: `int i_base_k = (j_base + padding - k * dilation) / stride`. Since `j_base` is a kernel argument and k is small, this division happens once per thread and is cheap. Then: `int i = i_base_k + tid;` `if (i < 0 || i >= L_in) continue;` Since only first/last few threads have invalid i, branch divergence is minimal. Now, we need to load x into shared memory. The i range for the block: `block_start_tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x;` `i_min_block = min_k (i_base_k + block_start_tid);` `i_max_block = max_k (i_base_k + block_start_tid + blockDim.x - 1);` `i_min_clamped = max(0, i_min_block);` `i_max_clamped = min(L_in - 1, i_max_block);` `i_count = i_max_clamped - i_min_clamped + 1;` We need shared memory for x and w. w can be loaded as before. x_smem size = C_in * max_i_count. max_i_count = blockDim.x + max_off + 1, where max_off = max_k(i_base_k) - min_k(i_base_k). Since i_base_k are constants independent of block, max_off is constant. For our case, max_off = 2. So allocate dynamic shared memory = C_in * max_i_count + blockDim.y * C_in * K. In kernel: ```cpp int block_start_tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x; int i_min_block = i_base_min + block_start_tid; // i_base_min = min_k i_base_k int i_max_block = i_base_max + block_start_tid + blockDim.x - 1; int i_min_load = max(0, i_min_block); int i_max_load = min(L_in - 1, i_max_block); int i_count = i_max_load - i_min_load + 1; ``` But `i_base_min` and `i_base_max` are constants. We can compute `i_min_block` and `i_max_block` in kernel. However, if we want to avoid per-thread computation of min/max, we can have the host pass `i_base_min` and `i_base_max` and `max_i_count` as constants. Let's compute on host: ``` int i_base_min = INF, i_base_max = -INF; for k in 0..K-1: int i_base = (j_base + padding - k*dilation) / stride; i_base_min = min(i_base_min, i_base); i_base_max = max(i_base_max, i_base); int max_off = i_base_max - i_base_min; ``` Then in kernel, a block's i range is [i_base_min + block_start_tid, i_base_max + block_start_tid + blockDim.x - 1] intersected with [0, L_in-1]. max_i_count = blockDim.x + max_off + 1 (maybe +1 for rounding). Actually i_base_max - i_base_min + blockDim.x. For blockDim.x=64, max_off=2: max_i_count = 64+2 = 66. But because of inclusive bounds: i_count = (i_base_max + tid_max) - (i_base_min + tid_min) + 1 = max_off + blockDim.x - 1 + 1 = max_off + blockDim.x. So `max_i_count = blockDim.x + max_off`. Wait, for interior: i_min_block = 0 + block_start. i_max_block = 2 + block_start + 63 = block_start + 65. i_count = 66. max_off = 2. blockDim.x = 64. So max_i_count = 64 + 2 = 66. Exactly. What about blockDim.x = 64, block_start = 0. i_min = -1, i_max = 65. i_count_loaded = 0..65 = 66. Same. So `max_i_count = blockDim.x + max_off` is correct because i_base_max - i_base_min = max_off, and the range spans `max_off + (blockDim.x - 1) + 1` = `blockDim.x + max_off` when not clamped. When clamped at boundaries, it's smaller. But allocation uses max_i_count. So shared memory size = C_in * (tile_t + max_off) + tile_c_out * C_in * K. Now, x_smem indexing: For a given c_in and i_global valid: `x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + (i_global - i_min_load)]` where i_min_load = max(0, i_min_block). In compute loop: ``` for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = i_base[k] + tid; if (i >= 0 && i < L_in) { float x_val = x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + (i - i_min_load)]; float w_val = w_smem[(threadIdx.y * C_in + c_in) * K + k]; sum += x_val * w_val; } } } ``` Here, `i_base[k]` is computed per block or passed in. Since it's constant, we can pass it as a kernel argument `int i_base_k`. For K up to maybe 7, we can pass them individually. Let's see: in the C++ wrapper, we have an array `i_base[8]`. We can pass them to the kernel by adding them as arguments: `int i0, int i1, int i2`. But we want to template on K. We can just compute them inside the kernel: `int i_base_k = (j_base + padding - k * dilation) / stride;`. Since `j_base` and `padding` and `dilation` and `stride` are kernel arguments, the compiler will compute them with integer division. For constant stride known at compile time? No, stride is runtime. But integer division by a runtime value is okay. It's only done once per thread, not inside the inner loop. The inner loop just does `int i = i_base_k + tid;` which is an add. The inner loop also does `i - i_min_load`, but `i_min_load` depends on `i_base_min + block_start_tid`, which can be computed once per thread before the loops. So the kernel is very efficient. Let's write this kernel. Host side C++ wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor weight, torch::optional<torch::Tensor> bias_opt, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { int N = x.size(0); int C_in = x.size(1); int L_in = x.size(2); int C_out = weight.size(0); int K = weight.size(2); int L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (K - 1) + 1; auto out = torch::zeros({N, C_out, L_out}, x.options()); if (L_out <= 0) return out; // Determine if we can use fast path bool use_fast = true; int r0 = ((0 * dilation - padding) % stride + stride) % stride; for (int k = 1; k < K; ++k) { int r = ((k * dilation - padding) % stride + stride) % stride; if (r != r0) { use_fast = false; break; } } const int tile_t = 128; const int tile_c_out = 8; dim3 block(tile_t, tile_c_out); if (use_fast) { int j_base = -padding + r0; // first j in residue class, might be negative // Actually j_base should be the smallest j >= 0 such that j % stride == r0 and there is at least one valid i. // The first valid j for any k is when i=0: j = k*d - p. The minimum over k of k*d - p may be negative. // We need the smallest j >= 0 with j % stride == r0 and j >= min_k(k*d - p) and j <= max_k(k*d - p + stride*(L_in-1)) int j_min_full = 0; int j_max_full = L_out - 1; int j_start = j_min_full; while (j_start % stride != r0) j_start++; int j_end = j_max_full; while (j_end % stride != r0) j_end--; int num_j = (j_end - j_start) / stride + 1; if (num_j > 0) { // Compute i_base_min and max_off int i_base_max = -1000000000; int i_base_min = 1000000000; for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i_base = (j_start + padding - k * dilation) / stride; if (i_base < i_base_min) i_base_min = i_base; if (i_base > i_base_max) i_base_max = i_base; } int max_off = i_base_max - i_base_min; int max_i_count = tile_t + max_off; size_t smem_size = (C_in * max_i_count + tile_c_out * C_in * K) * sizeof(float); dim3 grid((num_j + tile_t - 1) / tile_t, (C_out + tile_c_out - 1) / tile_c_out, N); // launch kernel // ... } } // else generic ... } ``` Wait, `j_start` computation: `j_start` must be such that for the active ks, `i` is within `[0, L_in-1]` for at least one k. But our `num_j` currently includes boundary j's where some k are invalid. That's fine because the kernel checks `i >= 0 && i < L_in`. However, `j_start` might be 0 or 1. For our case, r0 = ((-1)%2+2)%2 = 1. j_start starts at 0, increments to 1. j_end = 262144, decrements to 262143. num_j = (262143-1)/2+1 = 131072. i_base for k=0: (1+1-0)/2 = 1. i_base for k=1: (1+1-2)/2 = 0. i_base for k=2: (1+1-4)/2 = -1. i_base_min = -1, i_base_max = 1. max_off = 2. max_i_count = 128 + 2 = 130. x_smem = 32*130 = 4160 floats = 16.6KB. w_smem = 8*32*3 = 768 = 3KB. Total = 19.6KB. Fine. But note: for block 0, i_min_block = -1. i_min_load = 0. i_count = i_max_load - 0 + 1. For block 0: i_max_block = 1 + 0 + 127 = 128. i_max_load = 128 (clamped to L_in-1? L_in=131072, so yes). i_count = 129. max_i_count is 130, so x_smem has enough space. Compute for thread tid=0 (j=1): i for k=0: 1. offset in smem: 1 - 0 = 1. i for k=1: 0. offset: 0. i for k=2: -1. Skip (bounds check). So x_smem[1] and x_smem[0] are used. x_smem index for i=-1 is not accessed. Good. Now, tile_t=128, tile_c_out=8. block=1024. Grid: num_tiles = ceil(131072 / 128) = 1024. c_out_tiles = ceil(64/8) = 8. batch=16. Total blocks = 1024 * 8 * 16 = 131072 blocks. Each block does 128*8*96 FMAs = 98304 FMAs. Total FLOPs = 131072 * 98304 ≈ 12.89 B. Correct. Memory: x reads per block: C_in * i_count ≈ 32*129 = 4128 floats = 16.5KB. w reads: 8*32*3 = 768 floats = 3KB. output writes: 128*8 = 1024 floats = 4KB. Total memory traffic per block ≈ 23.5KB. For 131072 blocks = 3GB. For 12.9B FMAs, arithmetic intensity = 12.9B / 3GB ≈ 4.3 FLOP/byte. That's memory bound! Wait, 3GB traffic, but the input x is 256MB, weight is 24KB, output is 512MB (since half is zero, but we write all non-zero positions). So total unique data is ~768MB. But because of blocking, x is read multiple times (once per c_out tile). With tile_c_out=8, each x element is read by 8 blocks (one per c_out tile). So total x traffic = 256MB * 8 = 2GB. Output traffic = 512MB. Weight traffic = 24KB * c_out_tiles * batch * t_tiles? Weight is read once per block? In our kernel, weight is loaded into shared memory per block. There are 131072 blocks. Each block loads 3KB of weight. Total weight traffic = 131072 * 3KB = 384MB. Wait, but weight is only 24KB total! We are reading it 16384 times (131072 blocks / 8 c_out per block = 16384 c_out tiles? Actually, each block loads weight for 8 c_out channels. There are 8 c_out tiles. So each weight element is loaded once per batch and once per t_tile? Let's see: block grid is (1024, 8, 16). For a fixed c_out tile and batch, there are 1024 t tiles. Each of those blocks loads the same weight for its c_out tile. So weight is read 1024 times per c_out tile per batch. There are 8 c_out tiles * 16 batch = 128 block groups. Each group loads 3KB. Total weight reads = 128 * 1024 * 3KB = 384MB. But weight is tiny and stays in L2 cache. So actual DRAM traffic for weight is only 24KB. The rest is L2 cache hits. Good. Similarly, x is read by many blocks, but if tile_c_out=8 and t_tiles are sequential, x might stay in L2. However, each x element is read by 8 c_out tiles * 16 batch? Wait, x is per batch. For a given batch element, x is read by all c_out tiles (8) and all t tiles (1024). But each t tile reads a different slice. So each x element is read by 8 blocks (one per c_out tile). L2 can hold 40MB. x is 16MB per batch (256MB / 16). So L2 can hold the entire x for a batch. Thus x is read from DRAM once per batch. Total x DRAM traffic = 256MB. Output is written once: 512MB. So total DRAM traffic ≈ 768MB. FLOPs = 12.9B. Arithmetic intensity = 12.9B / 0.768GB ≈ 16.8 FLOPs/byte. Ridge point for A100 is maybe 5-10. So this is compute-bound! Great. The kernel should be fast if we achieve good compute utilization. Now, we need to map this to CUDA. The only issue is writing a robust generic fast path. Since the generic fallback is only needed for strange parameters, we can write a simple one. But wait, if weight stays in L2 and x stays in L2, then the kernel is compute-bound. The main limiter is instruction throughput. We need to ensure the inner loop is efficient. Inner loop: ```cpp for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { float x0 = x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + (i0 - i_min_load)]; float x1 = x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + (i1 - i_min_load)]; float x2 = x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + (i2 - i_min_load)]; sum0 += x0 * w0; sum1 += x1 * w1; sum2 += x2 * w2; } ``` Wait, we can unroll K. For K=3, we can compute partial sums for each k and then combine? Actually, we need to write to different j's: out_j0, out_j1, out_j2. Since each k maps to a different j, we need separate accumulators. ```cpp float accum[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) accum[k] = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = i_base[k] + tid; if (i >= 0 && i < L_in) { float xv = x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + (i - i_min_load)]; float wv = w_smem[(threadIdx.y * C_in + c_in) * K + k]; accum[k] += xv * wv; } } } // write accum[k] to out[b, c_out, j_k] ``` This is good. The `if (i >= 0 && i < L_in)` is inside the inner loop, but for K=3 it's okay. For interior threads, it's always true, and the branch is uniformly taken, so no divergence. However, the `i - i_min_load` computation is an integer subtract. The `x_smem` indexing involves a multiply `c_in * max_i_count`. Since `max_i_count` is constant within the kernel (dynamic, but same for all blocks), the compiler can strength-reduce it, but it's still an integer mul per inner iteration. We can hoist the base pointer: ```cpp float* x_row = x_smem + c_in * max_i_count; int off = i - i_min_load; float xv = x_row[off]; ``` This is better. Also, `w_smem` indexing: `float* w_ptr = w_smem + (threadIdx.y * C_in + c_in) * K;` then `wv = w_ptr[k];`. Since `threadIdx.y * C_in + c_in` changes with c_in, we can update the pointer each iteration. So the inner loop: ```cpp float* w_base = w_smem + threadIdx.y * C_in * K; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { float* x_row = x_smem + c_in * max_i_count; float* w_ptr = w_base + c_in * K; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = i_base[k] + tid; if (i >= 0 && i < L_in) { accum[k] += x_row[i - i_min_load] * w_ptr[k]; } } } ``` But `i_base[k] + tid` and `i - i_min_load` are the same for all c_in. We can precompute the offsets `off_k = i_base[k] + tid - i_min_load`. Since `i_min_load` is constant per block (not per thread? Wait, `i_min_load` depends on `block_start_tid` and `i_base_min`. It is the same for all threads in the block. So `off_k` varies per thread and k, but not per c_in. So we can precompute it before the c_in loop. ```cpp int off_k[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = i_base[k] + tid; off_k[k] = i - i_min_load; } // Then inside loop: for c_in ... for k ... if (i_base[k] + tid >= 0 && i_base[k] + tid < L_in) { accum[k] += x_row[off_k[k]] * w_ptr[k]; } ``` But the bounds check is the same for all c_in. We can precompute a boolean `valid_k`: ```cpp bool valid_k[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = i_base[k] + tid; valid_k[k] = (i >= 0 && i < L_in); off_k[k] = i - i_min_load; } ``` Then the inner loop is branch-free (for interior blocks, all valid_k are true). This is excellent. So the kernel becomes: ```cpp template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int j_base, int num_j, int i_base_min, int i_base_max) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (tid >= num_j || c_out >= C_out) return; int j = j_base + tid * stride; // Precompute i for each k int i_base[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { i_base[k] = (j_base + padding - k * dilation) / stride; } // Actually j_base is constant, so i_base is constant across threads. We could pass it from host. // But computing once per thread is fine. int block_start_tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x; int i_min_block = i_base_min + block_start_tid; int i_max_block = i_base_max + block_start_tid + blockDim.x - 1; int i_min_load = max(0, i_min_block); int i_max_load = min(L_in - 1, i_max_block); int i_count = i_max_load - i_min_load + 1; int max_i_count = blockDim.x + (i_base_max - i_base_min); // Shared mem extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_smem = smem; float* w_smem = smem + C_in * max_i_count; int block_size = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; // Load x for (int idx = tidx; idx < C_in * i_count; idx += block_size) { int c_in = idx / i_count; int i_local = idx % i_count; int i_global = i_min_load + i_local; x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + i_local] = x[((b * C_in + c_in) * L_in) + i_global]; } // Load w int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y; int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K; for (int idx = tidx; idx < w_size; idx += block_size) { int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < C_out) { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k]; } else { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Precompute offsets and validity int off_k[K]; bool valid_k[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = i_base[k] + tid; valid_k[k] = (i >= 0 && i < L_in); off_k[k] = i - i_min_load; // may be negative for invalid, but we won't use it } float accum[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) accum[k] = 0.0f; float* w_base = w_smem + threadIdx.y * C_in * K; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { float* x_row = x_smem + c_in * max_i_count; float* w_ptr = w_base + c_in * K; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { if (valid_k[k]) { accum[k] += x_row[off_k[k]] * w_ptr[k]; } } } if (bias != nullptr) { float bval = bias[c_out]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { if (valid_k[k]) { accum[k] += bval; } } } // Write output #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { if (valid_k[k]) { int j_out = j_base + tid * stride + k * dilation - padding; // Actually j = i*stride + k*d - p. Wait, j = j_base + tid*stride. But j_base already incorporates the residue. // j_out = j + (k*dilation - padding) - (j_base + padding - k*dilation)/stride * stride? No. // Actually j is the output position for this thread. But we have K accumulators, each corresponds to a different j. // Wait! In our mapping, each thread handles one j? No, each thread handles one tid in the progression, but there are K accumulators for K different j's? No. // Let's revisit the mapping. ``` Wait, I made a conceptual error. In the fast kernel, I was going to have each thread compute one j in the progression, but with K accumulators? No. In the gather kernel, each thread computes one output element out[b, c_out, j] = sum_{c_in, valid k} x[b, c_in, i_k] * w[c_out, c_in, k]. For a given j, there can be multiple k that are valid. For our case, K=3 and all are valid. So the thread computes a sum of 3 terms per c_in. The result is a single scalar written to out[b, c_out, j]. But in the transposed conv formula, out[j] = sum_{c_in} [ x[i_0]*w0 + x[i_1]*w1 + x[i_2]*w2 ]. Yes, that's a single sum for each j. I mistakenly thought each k writes to a different j. No! In the gather approach, each thread computes one j, summing over all k that map to that j. So there is only one accumulator per thread, not K. I confused the scatter and gather approaches. In scatter, each input i maps to K output j's. In gather, each output j gathers from K input i's. So in the fast kernel, each thread computes one j and loops over c_in and k, accumulating into one sum. Then writes one output value. This simplifies things a lot! There's no atomicAdd needed. For a given j = j_base + tid*stride, the valid ks are those where i_k = (j + padding - k*dilation)/stride is integer and in range. Since we are in a single residue class where all ks are active and j is chosen from that class, (j + padding - k*dilation) is divisible by stride for all k. So i_k = i_base[k] + tid, where i_base[k] = (j_base + padding - k*dilation)/stride. Then the sum is: for c_in in 0..C_in-1: sum += x[c_in, i0] * w[c_out, c_in, k0] + x[c_in, i1] * w[c_out, c_in, k1] + ... So each thread does C_in * K FMAs into one accumulator. Then writes to out[b, c_out, j]. So the kernel has: - One accumulator per thread. - Reads K values from x_smem per c_in. - Reads K values from w_smem per c_in. This is even better. For our case, K=3. So per c_in, we load 3 x values and 3 w values. The 3 x values are at off_k[0], off_k[1], off_k[2] in x_row. These are contiguous or near contiguous (since i differs by 1). We can load them with 3 separate loads. If x_smem is in shared memory, bank conflicts could occur if the offsets map to the same bank. Shared memory banks are 32 bits wide. offset differs by 1, so they are in adjacent banks, no conflict. So the kernel: ```cpp template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int j_base, int num_j, int i_base_min, int i_base_max) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (tid >= num_j || c_out >= C_out) return; int j = j_base + tid * stride; int i_base[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { i_base[k] = (j_base + padding - k * dilation) / stride; } int block_start_tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x; int i_min_block = i_base_min + block_start_tid; int i_max_block = i_base_max + block_start_tid + blockDim.x - 1; int i_min_load = max(0, i_min_block); int i_max_load = min(L_in - 1, i_max_block); int i_count = i_max_load - i_min_load + 1; int max_i_count = blockDim.x + (i_base_max - i_base_min); extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_smem = smem; float* w_smem = smem + C_in * max_i_count; int block_size = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; // Load x for (int idx = tidx; idx < C_in * i_count; idx += block_size) { int c_in = idx / i_count; int i_local = idx % i_count; int i_global = i_min_load + i_local; x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + i_local] = x[((b * C_in + c_in) * L_in) + i_global]; } // Load w int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y; int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K; for (int idx = tidx; idx < w_size; idx += block_size) { int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < C_out) { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k]; } else { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Precompute x offsets int off_k[K]; bool valid_k[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = i_base[k] + tid; valid_k[k] = (i >= 0 && i < L_in); off_k[k] = i - i_min_load; } float sum = 0.0f; float* w_base = w_smem + threadIdx.y * C_in * K; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { float* x_row = x_smem + c_in * max_i_count; float* w_ptr = w_base + c_in * K; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { if (valid_k[k]) { sum += x_row[off_k[k]] * w_ptr[k]; } } } if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[c_out]; } out[((b * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum; } ``` Wait, this computes the sum for j. It loops over all k and accumulates into one sum. That's correct! And it writes one value per thread. No atomics. The only thing: does each k correspond to the same j? Yes, j is fixed. The condition `j + padding - k*dilation` divisible by stride is satisfied for all k (since we chose j from the residue class). i_k may be out of bounds, but we handle that. So `j` is the output index. `i_base[k]` is the i when tid=0. For tid>0, i = i_base[k] + tid. This is correct because j = j_base + tid*stride, so: i = (j_base + tid*stride + padding - k*dilation)/stride = (j_base + padding - k*dilation)/stride + tid = i_base[k] + tid. Great. Now, we need to compute i_base_min and i_base_max on host. i_base_min = min_k i_base[k] i_base_max = max_k i_base[k] max_i_count = tile_t + i_base_max - i_base_min. But `i_base[k]` may be negative. For our case, i_base[2] = -1. i_base_min = -1. i_base_max = 1. max_off = 2. max_i_count = tile_t + 2. In the first block, block_start_tid=0. i_min_block = -1. i_max_block = 1 + 0 + tile_t - 1 = tile_t. So i_count loaded = min(L_in-1, tile_t) - max(0, -1) + 1 = tile_t + 1. But max_i_count = tile_t + 2, so x_smem is large enough. For thread tid=0: i = i_base[2] + 0 = -1. valid = false. i = i_base[1] + 0 = 0. off = 0 - 0 = 0. i = i_base[0] + 0 = 1. off = 1. Good. For thread tid=tile_t-1: i = -1 + 127 = 126 (valid). off = 126 - 0 = 126. max_i_count = 130. So off fits. For the last block, block_start_tid = num_j - tile_t. i_min_block = -1 + block_start. i_max_block = 1 + block_start + tile_t - 1 = block_start + tile_t. For L_in=131072, block_start up to 131072 - 128 = 130944. i_max_block = 130944 + 128 = 131072. i_max_load = min(131071, 131072) = 131071. i_min_load = 130943. i_count = 131071 - 130943 + 1 = 129. max_i_count = 130. So enough. For tid = 127 (last thread in block): i = 1 + 130944 + 127 = 131072 (invalid for k=0). valid=false. i = 0 + ... = 131071 (valid). i = -1 + ... = 131070 (valid). So it's correct. Now, what about j values? j = j_base + tid*stride. For our case, j_base = 1, stride=2, tid=131071 -> j = 1 + 262142 = 262143. That's less than L_out=262145. Good. The output is written to out[... + j]. For j=1,3,...,262143. This covers the non-zero positions. What about j=0 and j=262144? They are zero. But `out` was initialized to zeros, so they remain zero. Perfect. Now, tile_t needs to be chosen. Let's pick tile_t = 128, tile_c_out = 8. Block = 1024 threads. max_i_count = 128 + 2 = 130. x_smem = 32 * 130 = 4160 floats. w_smem = 8 * 32 * 3 = 768 floats. Total = 4928 floats = 19712 bytes ≈ 19.3KB. We allocate `shared_mem_size = (C_in * max_i_count + tile_c_out * C_in * K) * sizeof(float)`. This is constant per launch because max_i_count depends only on tile_t and K,d,s, which are known at launch time. Wait, but i_base_min and i_base_max are computed on host and passed. max_i_count = tile_t + (i_base_max - i_base_min). This is known before launch. So we can compute shared mem size and pass it. And for the fast path (single class), we launch the fast kernel. But we can also just always compute the progression for the case where all ks have the same residue. Let's write a simple detection. C++ wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { int N = x.size(0); int C_in = x.size(1); int L_in = x.size(2); int C_out = weight.size(0); int K = weight.size(2); int L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (K - 1) + 1; auto out = torch::zeros({N, C_out, L_out}, x.options()); const int tile_t = 128; const int tile_c_out = 8; // Check for fast path: all k share the same residue modulo stride bool use_fast = false; int r0 = 0; if (stride > 1 && K > 0) { r0 = ((0 * dilation - padding) % stride + stride) % stride; use_fast = true; for (int k = 1; k < K; ++k) { int r = ((k * dilation - padding) % stride + stride) % stride; if (r != r0) { use_fast = false; break; } } } if (use_fast) { // Find j_start: smallest j >= 0 with j % stride == r0 and j <= L_out-1 int j_start = 0; while (j_start < L_out && j_start % stride != r0) j_start++; int j_end = L_out - 1; while (j_end >= 0 && j_end % stride != r0) j_end--; int num_j = (j_end >= j_start) ? ((j_end - j_start) / stride + 1) : 0; if (num_j > 0) { int i_base_min = INT_MAX; int i_base_max = INT_MIN; std::vector<int> i_base(K); for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { i_base[k] = (j_start + padding - k * dilation) / stride; if (i_base[k] < i_base_min) i_base_min = i_base[k]; if (i_base[k] > i_base_max) i_base_max = i_base[k]; } int max_off = i_base_max - i_base_min; int max_i_count = tile_t + max_off; size_t smem_size = (C_in * max_i_count + tile_c_out * C_in * K) * sizeof(float); dim3 block(tile_t, tile_c_out); dim3 grid((num_j + tile_t - 1) / tile_t, (C_out + tile_c_out - 1) / tile_c_out, N); // Launch kernel switch (K) { case 1: conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel<1><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); break; case 2: conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel<2><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); break; case 3: conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel<3><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); break; case 4: conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel<4><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); break; case 5: conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel<5><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); break; case 6: conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel<6><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); break; case 7: conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel<7><<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); break; default: // fallback // generic kernel break; } C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK(); return out; } } // Generic fallback kernel dim3 block_generic(64, 8); dim3 grid_generic((L_out + 64 - 1) / 64, (C_out + 8 - 1) / 8, N); size_t smem_generic = (C_in * (64 + 10) + 8 * C_in * K) * sizeof(float); // rough switch (K) { case 1: conv_transpose1d_generic_kernel<1><<<grid_generic, block_generic, smem_generic>>>(...); break; ... } C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK(); return out; } ``` Wait, the generic fallback kernel also needs to be defined. Let's define a generic gather kernel without the fast path. Generic gather kernel: ```cpp template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_generic_kernel(...) { int j = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (j >= L_out || c_out >= C_out) return; int j_start = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x; int j_end = min(j_start + blockDim.x, L_out) - 1; int i_min = (j_start + padding - (K - 1) * dilation) / stride - 1; int i_max = (j_end + padding) / stride + 1; if (i_min < 0) i_min = 0; if (i_max >= L_in) i_max = L_in - 1; int i_count = i_max - i_min + 1; if (i_count < 0) i_count = 0; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_smem = smem; float* w_smem = smem + C_in * i_count; int block_size = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; for (int idx = tidx; idx < C_in * i_count; idx += block_size) { int c_in = idx / i_count; int i_local = idx % i_count; x_smem[c_in * i_count + i_local] = x[((b * C_in + c_in) * L_in) + i_min + i_local]; } int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y; int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K; for (int idx = tidx; idx < w_size; idx += block_size) { int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < C_out) { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k]; } else { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); float sum = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int num = j + padding - k * dilation; if (num % stride == 0) { int i = num / stride; if (i >= i_min && i <= i_max) { sum += x_smem[c_in * i_count + (i - i_min)] * w_smem[(threadIdx.y * C_in + c_in) * K + k]; } } } } if (bias != nullptr) sum += bias[c_out]; out[((b * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum; } ``` But the shared memory size for the generic kernel depends on `i_count` which varies per block. In CUDA dynamic shared memory, the size is fixed at launch. So we need to pass the maximum possible i_count for the generic kernel. max_i_count_generic = blockDim.x + ((K-1)*dilation + stride - 1)/stride + 2. Actually, for generic: j_end - j_start = blockDim.x - 1. i_max - i_min ≈ (blockDim.x - 1 + (K-1)*dilation)/stride + 2. So we can compute a safe upper bound. For simplicity, we can set `max_i_count = blockDim.x * 2 + K * dilation / stride + 4`. A safe over-approximation. To keep it simple, I'll use a fixed max_i_count for the generic kernel as well: `int max_i_count_generic = blockDim.x + K * dilation / stride + 4;` This is enough. Then inside the kernel, we use `i_count` for loops but allocate `max_i_count_generic` for smem. The indexing uses `i_count` for loading, but the actual smem stride should be `max_i_count_generic` because `i_count` varies. Wait, in the generic kernel code above, I used `x_smem[c_in * i_count + i_local]`. We can pass it as a kernel argument? Or compute it before launching. Since it's the same for all blocks in a launch? No, boundary blocks have smaller i_count. To avoid this complexity, for the generic kernel we can just not use shared memory for x. We can read x directly from global memory. Since the generic kernel is a fallback for small inputs or weird parameters, performance is less critical. We can still use shared memory for w (which has fixed size per block: tile_c_out * C_in * K). For x, we read directly from global memory. Generic kernel without x_smem: ```cpp template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_generic_kernel(...) { int j = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (j >= L_out || c_out >= C_out) return; // load w into smem extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* w_smem = smem; int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y; int block_size = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K; for (int idx = tidx; idx < w_size; idx += block_size) { int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < C_out) { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k]; } else { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); float sum = 0.0f; float* w_ptr = w_smem + threadIdx.y * C_in * K; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int num = j + padding - k * dilation; if (num % stride == 0) { int i = num / stride; if (i >= 0 && i < L_in) { float x_val = x[((b * C_in + c_in) * L_in) + i]; float w_val = w_ptr[c_in * K + k]; sum += x_val * w_val; } } } } if (bias != nullptr) sum += bias[c_out]; out[((b * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum; } ``` This is much simpler and still correct. The w_smem is small. x reads are coalesced if threads in a warp have the same c_in and k but different j? Wait, in a warp (fixed c_out, varying j), for a given k, the i = (j + p - k*d)/s.
