Therefore, in our kernel, `weight` is `[C_in, C_out, K]`, not `[C_out, C_in, K]`. This changes indexing! In our kernel, `w[c_out, c_in, k]` is wrong. It should be `w[c_in, c_out, k]`. Let's adjust: - `w_smem` indexing: `w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k]` corresponds to layout `[c_out, c_in, K]`. But if weight is `[c_in, c_out, K]`, the indexing should be `w[((c_in * C_out + c_out) * K) + k]`. - In shared memory, to have coalesced access per c_out, we should store `w_smem` as `[c_out_local][c_in][k]` or `[c_out_local][k][c_in]`. But if weight is `[c_in, c_out, K]`, then for fixed c_out and varying c_in, the weight is strided by C_out*K. Wait, if weight is `[C_in, C_out, K]`, then: - `w[c_in, c_out, k]` for fixed c_out, varying c_in, K: not contiguous. The stride between c_in values is C_out*K. - For fixed c_in, varying c_out, K: stride is K. So the memory layout is transposed compared to what we assumed. In our fast kernel, each thread has fixed c_out and loops over c_in. So it accesses `w[c_in, c_out, k]` for varying c_in. This is strided by C_out*K. That's not contiguous. We have two options: 1. Transpose the weight on the host before passing to the kernel. 2. Adjust the kernel to read weight efficiently. Option 1 is easier: in `ModelNew.forward()`, we can do `weight_t = self.conv1d_transpose.weight.permute(1, 0, 2).contiguous()`. Then pass `weight_t` to the kernel, where it is `[C_out, C_in, K]`. This adds a small overhead (permute is usually a view, but contiguous may copy). However, since weight is small (64*32*3=6144 floats), the overhead is negligible. But if we want to avoid copying, we can read the transposed weight into shared memory efficiently. In the load loop, we can have threads load in a coalesced manner. Since weight is [C_in, C_out, K], and we need w_smem as [C_out_local, C_in, K] (to have contiguous w_ptr per c_out), the load is a transpose. Load pattern: Linearized idx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x. Map idx to (c_out_local, c_in, k). For coalesced load from global weight (C_in, C_out, K): Global address for (c_in, c_out_global, k). We want adjacent threads in a warp to access adjacent global memory. In the previous design, idx mapped to (c_out_local, c_in, k) with c_out_local changing slowest. For weight layout [C_in, C_out, K], adjacent global elements are for (c_in, c_out, k) with k fastest, then c_out, then c_in. If we set idx mapping: k fastest, then c_in, then c_out_local. Then adjacent threads access same c_out_local, same c_in, varying k -> contiguous. Good. But then w_smem layout is [c_out_local][c_in][k] (k contiguous). That's exactly what we want for w_ptr. So we can change the load mapping: ```cpp for (int idx = tidx; idx < w_size; idx += block_size) { int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < C_out) { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_in * C_out + c_out_global) * K) + k]; } else { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f; } } ``` Wait, is this coalesced? Let's see. For a warp, tidx varies by 1. k = tidx % K. For K=3, k cycles 0,1,2,0,1,2... The global address is `((c_in * C_out + c_out_global) * K) + k`. Since k changes fastest, adjacent threads (with same c_in, c_out) access adjacent k -> contiguous. Good. But when tidx increases by 3, c_in increments by 1. Then the address jumps by C_out*K. Not contiguous across that boundary. But warp processes 32 threads. With K=3, the warp covers 10 full (c_in,c_out) groups and 2 extra. So memory access is mostly contiguous within segments of size 3. This might be okay. Alternatively, we could transpose the weight first in Python. That might be simpler and the performance difference negligible. I think the simplest and safest is to permute the weight in Python to `[C_out, C_in, K]`. Since the weight size is tiny, the copy cost is negligible. So in `ModelNew.forward`: ```python w = self.conv1d_transpose.weight.permute(1, 0, 2).contiguous() ``` And then treat it as `[C_out, C_in, K]` in the kernel. Since the code is long, I need to be careful with string quoting. Also, we need to handle the case where `bias` is None. We pass a null pointer. Code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <vector> #include <climits> template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int j_base, int num_j, int i_base_min, int i_base_max) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (tid >= num_j || c_out >= C_out) return; int j = j_base + tid * stride; int i_base[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { i_base[k] = (j_base + padding - k * dilation) / stride; } int block_start_tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x; int i_min_block = i_base_min + block_start_tid; int i_max_block = i_base_max + block_start_tid + blockDim.x - 1; int i_min_load = (i_min_block > 0) ? i_min_block : 0; int i_max_load = (i_max_block < L_in - 1) ? i_max_block : (L_in - 1); int i_count = i_max_load - i_min_load + 1; if (i_count < 0) i_count = 0; int max_off = i_base_max - i_base_min; int max_i_count = blockDim.x + max_off; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_smem = smem; float* w_smem = smem + C_in * max_i_count; int block_size = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; // Load x tile for (int idx = tidx; idx < C_in * i_count; idx += block_size) { int c_in = idx / i_count; int i_local = idx % i_count; int i_global = i_min_load + i_local; x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + i_local] = x[((b * C_in + c_in) * L_in) + i_global]; } // Load w tile int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y; int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K; for (int idx = tidx; idx < w_size; idx += block_size) { int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < C_out) { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k]; } else { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Precompute validity and offsets bool valid_k[K]; int off_k[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i = i_base[k] + tid; valid_k[k] = (i >= 0 && i < L_in); off_k[k] = i - i_min_load; } float sum = 0.0f; float* w_base = w_smem + threadIdx.y * C_in * K; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { float* x_row = x_smem + c_in * max_i_count; float* w_ptr = w_base + c_in * K; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { if (valid_k[k]) { sum += x_row[off_k[k]] * w_ptr[k]; } } } if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[c_out]; } out[((b * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum; } // Generic fallback kernel (handles arbitrary parameters) __global__ void conv_transpose1d_generic_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { int j = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (j >= L_out || c_out >= C_out) return; // Load weight tile into shared memory int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y; int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* w_smem = smem; int block_size = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int tidx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; for (int idx = tidx; idx < w_size; idx += block_size) { int tmp = idx; int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K; int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in; int c_out_local = tmp; int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local; if (c_out_global < C_out) { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k]; } else { w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); float sum = 0.0f; float* w_ptr = w_smem + threadIdx.y * C_in * K; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { for (int k = 0; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose1d_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation) { int N = x.size(0); int C_in = x.size(1); ((j_end - j_start) / stride + 1) : 0; if (num_j > 0) { int i_base_min = INT_MAX; int i_base_max = INT_MIN; for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int i_base = (j_start + padding - k * dilation) / stride; if (i_base < i_base_min) i_base_min = i_base; if (i_base > i_base_max) i_base_max = i_base; } int max_off = i_base_max - i_base_min; int max_i_count = tile_t + max_off; size_t smem_size = (C_in * max_i_count + tile_c_out * C_in * K) * sizeof(float); if (smem_size <= 48 * 1024) { dim3 grid((num_j + tile_t - 1) / tile_t, (C_out + tile_c_out - 1) / tile_c_out, N); switch (K) { case 1: launch_fast<1>(x_ptr, w_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr, N, C_in, L_in, C_out, L_out, stride, padding, dilation, j_start, num_j, i_base_min, i_base_max, grid, block, smem_size, stream); break; case 2: launch_fast<2>(...); break; case 3: launch_fast<3>(...); break; case 4: launch_fast<4>(...); break; case 5: launch_fast<5>(...); break; case 6: launch_fast<6>(...); break; case 7: launch_fast<7>(...); break; default: use_fast = false; break; } if (use_fast) { C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK(); return out; } } } } // Fallback generic kernel dim3 grid_fallback((L_out + tile_t - 1) / tile_t, (C_out + tile_c_out - 1) / tile_c_out, N); size_t smem_fallback = (tile_c_out * C_in * K) * sizeof(float); conv_transpose1d_generic_kernel<<<grid_fallback, block, smem_fallback, stream>>>( x_ptr, w_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr, N, C_in, L_in, C_out, L_out, K, stride, padding, dilation); C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK(); return out; } ''' ``` Wait, in the launch_fast switch, I used `...` to avoid repetition. Wait, in the fast kernel, `i_base` array is computed with `j_base`, not `j`. Since `j_base` is the first j in the progression, `i_base[k]` is the i for tid=0. Then `i = i_base[k] + tid`. That's correct. Now, what if `j_base` is not the actual first j in the progression but the first valid j? We computed `j_start` as the first j >= 0 with j % stride == r0. But is `j_start` guaranteed to be such that for all k, (j_start + p - k*d) % s == 0? Yes, because r0 = (k*d - p) % s for all k. And j_start % s == r0. So (j_start + p - k*d) % s = (r0 + p - k*d) % s = ((k*d - p) + p - k*d) % s = 0. So yes. But what if `j_start = 0` and `0 % s == r0`? Then `i_base[k] = (0 + p - k*d)/s`. If `p - k*d` is negative and divisible by s, it's fine. Now, write the code. I will write it cleanly and compile. One more thing: `load_inline` with `cpp_sources=""` might need to be omitted? Let me write the code now. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <c10/cuda/CUDAStream.h> #include <climits> template <int K> __global__ void conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int j_base, int num_j, int i_base_min, int i_base_max) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (tid >= num_j || c_out >= C_out) return; int j = j_base + tid * stride; int i_base[K]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { i_base[k] = (j_base + padding - k * dilation) / stride; k < K; ++k) { if (valid_k[k]) { sum += x_row[off_k[k]] * w_ptr[k]; } } } if (bias != nullptr) { sum += bias[c_out]; } out[((b * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum; } __global__ void conv_transpose1d_generic_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { int j = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (j >= L_out || c_out >= C_out) return; int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y; int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K; extern __shared__ float smem[]; } out[((b * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum; } template <int K> void launch_fast(const float* x, const float* w, const float* bias, float* out, int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int j_base, int num_j, int i_base_min, int i_base_max, dim3 grid, dim3 block, size_t smem_size, cudaStream_t stream) { conv_transpose1d_fast_kernel<K><<<grid, block, smem_size, stream>>>( x, w, bias, out, N, C_in, L_in, C_out, L_out, stride, padding, dilation, j_base, num_j, i_base_min, i_base_max); So: Conv1d output at t = sum_k x[t + k] * w[k] For our transpose case: out[2t+1] = x[t+1] * w[0] + x[t] * w[1] + x[t-1] * w[2] If we define x_padded with appropriate padding and compute a regular Conv1d: conv_out[t] = x_padded[t] * w[0] + x_padded[t+1] * w[1] + x_padded[t+2] * w[2] = x[t-1] * w[0] + x[t] * w[1] + x[t+1] * w[2] where x_padded is padded on left by 1. Then: out[2t+1] = conv_out[t+1] ... no wait. Let me be more careful. Let's use conv1d with flipped weight. If we flip the weight: w_flipped[k] = w[K-1-k]: conv1d(x_padded, w_flipped)[t] = x_padded[t] * w[2] + x_padded[t+1] * w[1] + x_padded[t+2] * w[0] With x_padded having left pad 1: = x[t-1] * w[2] + x[t] * w[1] + x[t+1] * w[0] But we want: out[2t+1] = x[t+1] * w[0] + x[t] * w[1] + x[t-1] * w[2] = conv1d(x_padded, w_flipped)[t] So yes, out[2t+1] = conv1d(x_padded, w_flipped)[t]! For regular conv, if we want convolution (not cross-correlation), we'd need to flip the kernel. But we're already doing that here. So the efficient implementation is: 1. Flip the weight: w_flip = weight.flip(2) # shape (C_in, C_out, 3) -> (C_in, C_out, 3) 2. Pad x: x_pad = F.pad(x, (1, 1)) 3. Run regular conv1d: y = F.conv1d(x_pad, w_flip, stride=1) 4. Create output zeros, assign: out[..., 1::2] = y Wait, F.conv1d weight shape is (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size). Our weight from ConvTranspose1d is (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size) = (32, 64, 3). We need to permute and flip: w_for_conv = weight.permute(1, 0, 2).flip(2) # (64, 32, 3) Then F.conv1d(x_padded, w_for_conv) gives (N, 64, 131072). Then out[:, :, 1::2] = conv_result. out[:, :, ::2] = 0. But what about bias? F.conv1d supports bias. We'd just pass the bias. Wait, I need to verify the boundary behavior matches exactly. Let me try to write a Triton kernel that's general enough. Here's a simple Triton kernel idea: - Block size: (BLOCK_L_OUT, BLOCK_C_OUT) per batch - For each block, iterate over c_in in tiles and k in [0, K) - Each thread computes one (l_out, c_out) element - For each (c_in, k), check if the corresponding input position is valid, load x and w, accumulate But with triton, we can also fuse the output accumulation. For long sequences, we want to maximize memory bandwidth utilization. The operation is essentially a gather-scatter. Given the specific test case has L_out = 262145, which is very long, and small C_in=32, C_out=64, K=3, the kernel should have many parallel threads. Let me write a Triton kernel for this. Triton doesn't have the CXXABI issue. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def conv_transpose1d_kernel( x_ptr, w_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr, N, C_in, L_in, C_out, L_out, stride, padding, dilation, K, BLOCK_L: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_C_OUT: tl.constexpr, ): # Grid: (num_l_blocks, num_c_out_blocks, N) pid_l = tl.program_id(0) pid_c = tl.program_id(1) pid_b = tl.program_id(2) l_start = pid_l * BLOCK_L c_out_start = pid_c * BLOCK_C_OUT # Initialize accumulators acc = tl.zeros((BLOCK_L, BLOCK_C_OUT), dtype=tl.float32) for c_in in range(C_in): for k in range(K): # For each output position l in [l_start, l_start + BLOCK_L) # Compute valid input index # num = l + padding - k * dilation # if num % stride == 0: i = num // stride l_offs = l_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_L) mask_l = l_offs < L_out num = l_offs + padding - k * dilation valid = (num % stride == 0) & mask_l i = num // stride valid = valid & (i >= 0) & (i < L_in) # Load x values x_addr = ((pid_b * C_in + c_in) * L_in + i) x_vals = tl.load(x_ptr + x_addr, mask=valid, other=0.0) # Load w values for this (c_in, k) across c_out block # w shape after permute: (C_out, C_in, K) w_offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_C_OUT) w_mask = (c_out_start + w_offs) < C_out w_addr = ((c_out_start + w_offs) * C_in + c_in) * K + k w_vals = tl.load(w_ptr + w_addr, mask=w_mask, other=0.0) # Broadcast and accumulate # x_vals: (BLOCK_L,) # w_vals: (BLOCK_C_OUT,) acc += x_vals[:, None] * w_vals[None, :] # Add bias if bias_ptr is not None: bias_offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_C_OUT) bias_mask = (c_out_start + bias_offs) < C_out bias_vals = tl.load(bias_ptr + c_out_start + bias_offs, mask=bias_mask, other=0.0) acc += bias_vals[None, :] # Store output l_offs = l_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_L) c_out_offs = c_out_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_C_OUT) out_l_mask = l_offs < L_out out_c_mask = c_out_offs < C_out out_mask = out_l_mask[:, None] & out_c_mask[None, :] out_addr = ((pid_b * C_out + c_out_offs[None, :]) * L_out + l_offs[:, None]) tl.store(out_ptr + out_addr, acc, mask=out_mask) ``` Wait, Triton has some limitations. The address computation `out_addr` above uses broadcasting, which should work in newer Triton versions. Let me make sure. Also, `num % stride` where stride is a runtime variable might be tricky. In Triton, `%` with a variable divisor might not be fully supported or might be slow. But it should work. However, the `%` operation with a non-constant might generate expensive code. Since stride is small (common values 1, 2), it might be okay. For the specific test case where all (k * dilation - padding) % stride are equal, we could optimize further. Actually, `num % stride == 0` is equivalent to `(l + padding - k * dilation) % stride == 0`. In the general case, for a fixed k, the valid l's are spaced by `stride`. So only 1/stride of the l positions are valid for each k. This means for each (c_in, k), only about BLOCK_L/stride positions load valid x values. This is inefficient. To improve, instead of iterating over l in order, we could iterate over output blocks with step = stride. But this gets complicated. For the specific (and common) case where all k give the same remainder, we can structure the kernel to only compute the valid positions. For the general case, we can loop over l with stride step. Actually, I realize that for a general conv_transpose, there might always be a pattern. For each k, the valid j satisfy: j ≡ (k*dilation - padding) mod stride. So there are at most `stride` different residue classes. Each output position j can receive contributions from at most one k per residue class. If all k share the same residue class (like our test case), then only 1/stride of output positions receive any contribution at all. For the general case, there could be up to K different residue classes (or fewer if some coincide). A more efficient kernel would iterate over the residue classes. For each class r: - Find all k with residue r - Output positions j ≡ r (mod stride) receive contributions from these k's - We can write a kernel that only computes j in a specific residue class This would avoid checking `num % stride == 0` inside the inner loop. Let me write a two-level kernel: 1. Detect the unique residue classes 2. For each residue class, launch a kernel that computes output positions j = r, r+stride, r+2*stride, ... This is very similar to my C++ fast kernel. For a Triton implementation, I could just call PyTorch's `conv1d` on properly padded and indexed input. But for the general case... Actually for the general case, a solid approach is: 1. Reshape the operation as a series of strided convolutions 2. Or use the gather approach with precomputed masks Let me try the simple Triton kernel first and see if it's correct and fast enough. The benchmark might be dominated by memory bandwidth anyway. PyTorch wheels typically ship with their own libtorch.so and associated libraries. The issue might be that the compiled extension is linking against the system g++'s libstdc++. One common fix is to add `extra_cflags=['-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0']` to match PyTorch's compilation. But PyTorch itself might have been built with the newer ABI. reasoning
Hmm, the issue is the compiled extension being linked against a newer libstdc++ than the system one. This might be because `/opt/conda/lib` is not in the dynamic library search path when the extension is loaded inside the same Python process if `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` isn't already set that way. However, I set `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` early in the Python source! But that won't affect the current process's loader if it was already loaded. Wait, perhaps the issue is that `load_inline` uses the system linker default paths and picks up `/opt/conda/lib` at compile time but the *runtime* loader doesn't look there because the process was already started without it. Since `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` doesn't propagate back into the already running process's loader, I need to use `ctypes.CDLL` to explicitly load the newer `libstdc++.so.6` before importing the extension. This ensures the newer ABI is available. Let me try loading it via `ctypes` before the `load_inline` call.

reasoning
The compile tool seems to be running in a subprocess/fresh environment that doesn't load the custom libstdc++ workaround. The `extra_cflags=["-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0"]` isn't sufficient because the compiled .so still requires the newer libstdc++ symbols. A common real fix: compile with the exact same ABI as torch, which is 0 or 1? PyTorch in Conda uses ABI=1 (C++11 ABI). But the system libstdc++ is old (needs CXXABI_1.3.15). We need to link against /opt/conda/lib/libstdc++.so.6. We can pass extra linker flags to set RPATH but the subprocess likely doesn't find them. But if we use `extra_cuda_cflags` / `extra_cflags` / `extra_ldflags`, the `load_inline` will compile with those. We need to add `-Wl,-rpath,/opt/conda/lib` to `extra_ldflags`. Let's try `extra_ldflags=["-Wl,-rpath,/opt/conda/lib"]` with ABI not forced to 0.

For the given parameters (stride=2, dilation=2, kernel=3, padding=1), there might be a clever way to compute it using standard `conv1d` or `matmul`. Let's think about the math. ConvTranspose1d with kernel size K, stride s, dilation d, padding p. Output at index j is: sum_{c_in} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} x[c_in, i] * w[c_out, c_in, k] where i = (j + p - k*d) / s, only when (j + p - k*d) % s == 0 and i in [0, L_in-1]. For stride=2, dilation=2, padding=1, K=3. Then k*d = 0, 2, 4. j + 1 - k*2 must be even. For each j: - k=0: num = j+1. Must be even => j odd. - k=1: num = j+1-2 = j-1. Must be even => j odd. - k=2: num = j+1-4 = j-3. Must be even => j odd. So only odd j contribute! Even j get 0? Wait, if j is even, j+1 is odd, not divisible by 2. j-1 is odd, j-3 is odd. So for even j, no k satisfies the condition, so output[j] = bias only (or 0). For odd j: - k=0: i = (j+1)/2 - k=1: i = (j-1)/2 - k=2: i = (j-3)/2 So the transposed convolution essentially doubles the sequence length, with zeros at even positions (except bias), and at odd positions j=2t+1, the output is: sum_{c_in} [ x[c_in, t+1] * w[c_out, c_in, 0] + x[c_in, t] * w[c_out, c_in, 1] + x[c_in, t-1] * w[c_out, c_in, 2] ] where indices must be in bounds. This is equivalent to a standard Conv1d on the upsampled-by-2 input (with zeros inserted) with dilated kernel. But since only odd positions are non-zero, we can compute the output for odd positions using a regular convolution on the original input with stride 1 and appropriate padding. Specifically: Let y be the output. y_even = bias (or 0). y_odd[j=2t+1] = conv1d(x_padded)[t] where the conv has kernel [w0, w1, w2] and appropriate padding. Wait, let's derive: For j = 2t+1: i0 = (2t+1+1)/2 = t+1 i1 = (2t+1-1)/2 = t i2 = (2t+1-3)/2 = t-1 So y[:, :, 2t+1] = sum_{c_in} ( x[:, c_in, t+1] * w0 + x[:, c_in, t] * w1 + x[:, c_in, t-1] * w2 ) + bias. This looks like a standard Conv1d with kernel size 3, stride 1, padding=1 applied to x, but with the output indices mapped to odd positions. Let's check: Standard conv1d with stride=1, padding=1, kernel [w0, w1, w2]: output at t is sum_k sum_cin x[:, c_in, t + padding - k] * w_k. With padding=1, t -> x[:, c_in, t+1 - k]. For k=0: x[:, c_in, t+1] * w0 For k=1: x[:, c_in, t] * w1 For k=2: x[:, c_in, t-1] * w2 Exactly matches y[:, :, 2t+1]! So we can implement `ModelNew.forward` in pure PyTorch as: ```python def forward(self, x): # x: [N, C_in, L_in] y = F.conv1d(x, self.conv1d_transpose.weight, bias=None, stride=1, padding=1, dilation=1) # L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(K-1) + 1 L_in = x.shape[2] L_out = (L_in - 1) * self.stride - 2 * self.padding + self.dilation * (self.conv1d_transpose.kernel_size[0] - 1) + 1 out = torch.zeros(x.shape[0], self.conv1dtranspose.weight.shape[0], L_out, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) out[:, :, 1:2*L_in:2] = y if self.conv1d_transpose.bias is not None: out += self.conv1d_transpose.bias.view(1, -1, 1) return out ``` Wait, but PyTorch's `conv1d` is also implemented in cuDNN and should be very fast. The question is: will this be faster than the reference `ConvTranspose1d`? For the given parameters, the reference might be using a slower algorithm or might have additional overhead. idx < C_in * i_count; idx += block_size) {\n int c_in = idx / i_count;\n int i_local = idx % i_count;\n int i_global = i_min_load + i_local;\n x_smem[c_in * max_i_count + i_local] = x[((b * C_in + c_in) * L_in) + i_global];\n }\n\n // Load w tile\n int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y;\n int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K;\n for (int idx = tidx; idx < w_size; idx += block_size) {\n int tmp = idx;\n int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K;\n int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in;\n int c_out_local = tmp;\n int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local;\n if (c_out_global < C_out) {\n w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k];\n } else {\n w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n bool valid_k[K];\n int off_k[K];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) {\n int i = i_base[k] + tid;\n valid_k[k] = (i >= 0 && i < L_in);\n off_k[k] = i - i_min_load;\n }\n\n float sum = 0.0f;\n float* w_base = w_smem + threadIdx.y * C_in * K;\n for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) {\n float* x_row = x_smem + c_in * max_i_count;\n float* w_ptr = w_base + c_in * K;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) {\n if (valid_k[k]) {\n sum += x_row[off_k[k]] * w_ptr[k];\n }\n }\n }\n\n if (bias != nullptr) {\n sum += bias[c_out];\n }\n\n out[((b * C_out + c_out) * L_out) + j] = sum;\n}\n\n__global__ void conv_transpose1d_generic_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ x,\n const float* __restrict__ w,\n const float* __restrict__ bias,\n float* __restrict__ out,\n int N, int C_in, int L_in, int C_out, int L_out,\n int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation)\n{\n int j = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n int c_out = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y;\n int b = blockIdx.z;\n\n if (j >= L_out || c_out >= C_out) return;\n\n int c_out_base = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y;\n int w_size = blockDim.y * C_in * K;\n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* w_smem = smem;\n int block_size = blockDim.x * blockDim.y;\n int tidx = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x;\n for (int idx = tidx; k < K; ++k) {\n int r = ((k * dilation - padding) % stride + stride) % stride;\n if (r != r0) { use_fast = false; break; }\n }\n }\n\n if (use_fast) {\n int j_start = 0;\n while (j_start < L_out && j_start % stride != r0) j_start++;\n int j_end = L_out - 1;\n while (j_end >= 0 && j_end % stride != r0) j_end--;\n int num_j = (j_end >= j_start) ? idx < w_size; idx += block_size) {\n int tmp = idx;\n int k = tmp % K; tmp /= K;\n int c_in = tmp % C_in; tmp /= C_in;\n int c_out_local = tmp;\n int c_out_global = c_out_base + c_out_local;\n if (c_out_global < C_out) {\n w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = w[((c_out_global * C_in + c_in) * K) + k];\n } else {\n w_smem[(c_out_local * C_in + c_in) * K + k] = 0.0f;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n bool valid_k[K];\n int off_k[K];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) {\n int r = ((k * dilation - padding) % stride + stride) % stride;\n if (r != r0) { use_fast = false; break; }\n }\n }\n\n if (use_fast) {\n int j_start = 0;\n while (j_start < L_out && j_start % stride != r0) j_start++;\n int j_end = L_out - 1;\n while (j_end >= 0 && j_end % stride != r0) j_end--;\n int num_j = (j_end >= j_start) ? ((j_end - j_start) / stride + 1) : 0;\n\n if (num_j > 0) {\n int i_base_min = INT_MAX;\n int i_base_max = INT_MIN;\n for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) {\n int i_base = (j_start + padding - k * dilation) / stride;\n if (i_base < i_base_min) i_base_min = i_base;\n if (i_base > i_base_max) i_base_max = i_base;\n }\n int max_off = i_base_max - i_base_min;\n int max_i_count = tile_t + max_off;\n size_t smem_size = (C_in * max_i_count + tile_c_out * C_in * K) * sizeof(float);\n\n if (smem_size <= 48 * 1024) {\n dim3 grid((num_j + tile_t - 1) / tile_t, (C_out + tile_c_out - 1) / tile_c_out, N);\n switch (K) {\n case 1: launch_fast<1>(x_ptr, w_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr, N, C_in, L_in, C_out, L_out, stride, padding, dilation, j_start, num_j, i_base_min, i_base_max, grid, block, smem_size, stream); break;\n case 2: launch_fast<2>(x_ptr, w_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr, N, C_in, L_in, C_out, L_out, stride, padding, dilation, j_start, num_j, i_base_min, i_base_max, grid, block, smem_size, stream); break;\n case 3: launch_fast<3>(x_ptr, w_ptr, bias_ptr, out_ptr, N, C_in, L_in, C_out, L_out, stride, padding, dilation, j_start, num_j, i_base_min, i_base_max, grid, block, smem_size, stream); reasoning
Great, it compiled! Now let's run correctness. But before that, note: the reference `nn.ConvTranspose1d` with `in_channels=32, out_channels=64, kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1, dilation=2` uses weight of shape `(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size)` i.e. `(32, 64, 3)`. PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d formula gives output length: L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + 1 + output_padding Here output_padding defaults to 0. So L_out = (131072 - 1)*2 - 2*1 + 2*(3-1) + 1 = 262142 - 2 + 4 + 1 = 262145? Let me compute: (131072-1)=131071; *2 = 262142; -2 = 262140; +4 = 262144; +1 = 262145. Yes. Now my CUDA code receives weight with shape (C_out, C_in, K) because I permute. That's (64, 32, 3). Good. One issue: in my fast kernel I compute `i_base_min` and `i_base_max` using integer division rounding toward zero in C++. For negative values this could be wrong. Let's test with the problem parameters: stride=2 padding=1 dilation=2 kernel_size=3. r0 for k=0: ((0-1)%2+2)%2 = ((-1)%2+2)%2. In Python, -1%2 = 1. In C++, -1%2 = -1. So ((-1)+2)%2 = 1. Good. k=1: ((2-1)%2+2)%2 = (1+2)%2 = 1. Good. k=2: ((4-1)%2+2)%2 = (3%2+2)%2 = (1+2)%2 = 1. Good. So use_fast is true. j_start%2 should be 1. j_start=1. j_end: L_out-1 = 262144. 262144%2=0, so j_end=262143? Wait while j_end%2 != r0 (1) decrement. 262144%2=0, so j_end=262143. 262143%2=1 stop. num_j = (262143-1)/2 + 1 = 131072. Good. For k=0: i_base = (1 + 1 - 0)/2 = 1. For k=1: i_base = (1 + 1 - 2)/2 = 0. For k=2: i_base = (1 + 1 - 4)/2 = -1. Wait (1+1-4) = -2. /2 = -1. So i_base_min = -2? Actually wait: (1 + 1 - 0) / 2 = 1. (1 + 1 - 2) / 2 = 0. (1 + 1 - 4) / 2 = -2 / 2 = -1. i_base_max = 1. max_off = 2. max_i_count = 128 + 2 = 130. smem_size = (32 * 130 + 8 * 32 * 3) * 4 = (4160 + 768) * 4 = 4928 * 4 = 19712 bytes. Very small, well within 48KB. Great! Now i_min_block for block 0: i_base_min + 0 = -2. i_min_load = 0. i_max_block for block 0: i_base_max + 0 + 127 = 128. i_max_load = min(128, L_in-1) = 128. i_count = 129. max_i_count = 130. So x_smem: 32*130 floats = 4160. For thread 0: j=1. k=0: i=i_base[0]+0 = 1. valid. off=1-0=1. k=1: i=0+0=0. valid. off=0. k=2: i=-1+0=-1. invalid. Looks correct. Now what about block 1? block_start_tid=128. i_min_block = -2+128 = 126. i_min_load = 126. i_max_block = 1+128+127 = 256. i_max_load = min(256, 131071) = 256. For thread 0 (tid=128): j = 1 + 128*2 = 257. k=0: i = 1 + 128 = 129. valid. off = 129 - 126 = 3. k=1: i = 0 + 128 = 128. valid. off = 128 - 126 = 2. k=2: i = -1 + 128 = 127. valid. off = 127 - 126 = 1. Looks good. What about the tail block? last block: num_j=131072. grid = ceil(131072/128) = 1024 blocks. Block 1023: block_start_tid = 1023*128 = 130944. tid range = [130944, 131071]. j range = 1 + [130944, 131071]*2 = [261889, 262143]. All valid since L_out=262145. i_min_block = -2 + 130944 = 130942. i_min_load = 130942. i_max_block = 1 + 130944 + 127 = 131072. But L_in-1 = 131071. So i_max_load = 131071. i_count = 131071 - 130942 + 1 = 130. max_i_count = 130. For tid=130944: j=261889. k=0: i = 1 + 130944 = 130945. valid. off = 130945 - 130942 = 3. k=1: i = 0 + 130944 = 130944. valid. off = 2. k=2: i = -1 + 130944 = 130943. valid. off = 1. Good. For tid=131071: j=262143. i = 1 + 131071 = 131072. This is >= L_in=131072, so invalid. That's fine. So the fast kernel logic is correct. However, I use `(j_base + padding - k * dilation) / stride` with integer division in C++. For negative numerator, C++ integer division truncates toward zero. Here j_base=1, padding=1, so 1+1-4 = -2. -2/2 = -1. That's correct. What if parameters caused e.g. -1/2? That would give 0 in C++, but mathematically floor(-1/2) = -1. Would that be a problem? Let's see. If `j_start + padding - k*dilation = -1`, then actual `i_base` should be floor(-1/2) = -1? But in the convolution, `i` is defined by `j = i*stride + k*dilation - padding`. So `i = (j + padding - k*dilation) / stride`. Since `j % stride = r0`, and `r0 = (k*dilation - padding) % stride`, we have `j + padding - k*dilation` is exactly divisible by `stride`. So there's no remainder! The numerator is always a multiple of `stride`. Therefore integer division in C++ works fine. Good. Now test correctness.
